      <p><b>Iraq</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ٱلْعِرَاق</span>‎, <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">al-ʿIrāq</i></i>; <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish language">Kurdish</a>: <span lang="ku" dir="rtl">عێراق</span>‎ <i><i lang="ku-Latn" title="Kurdish-language romanization">Êraq</i></i>), officially the <b>Republic of Iraq</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">جُمْهُورِيَّة ٱلْعِرَاق</span>‎ <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization"><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ar-Jumh%C5%ABriyyat_al-%E2%80%98Ir%C4%81q.oga" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/Ar-Jumh%C5%ABriyyat_al-%E2%80%98Ir%C4%81q.oga" class="internal" title="Ar-Jumhūriyyat al-‘Irāq.oga">Jumhūriīyah al-ʿIrāq</a></span></span></i></i>; <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish language">Kurdish</a>: <span lang="ku" dir="rtl">کۆماری عێراق</span>‎ <i><i lang="ku-Latn" title="Kurdish-language romanization">Komarî Êraq</i></i>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, bordered by <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iraq%E2%80%93Turkey_border" title="Iraq–Turkey border">the north</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_border" title="Iran–Iraq border">the east</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iraq%E2%80%93Kuwait_border" title="Iraq–Kuwait border">the southeast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iraq%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_border" title="Iraq–Saudi Arabia border">the south</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iraq%E2%80%93Jordan_border" title="Iraq–Jordan border">the southwest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iraq%E2%80%93Syria_border" title="Iraq–Syria border">the west</a>. The capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>. Iraq is home to diverse ethnic groups including <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Turkmen" title="Iraqi Turkmen">Turkmens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yazidis" title="Yazidis">Yazidis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shabaks" title="Shabaks">Shabakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_Iraq" title="Armenians in Iraq">Armenians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Sabian-Mandaeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circassians_in_Iraq" title="Circassians in Iraq">Circassians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Romani_people_in_Iraq" title="Romani people in Iraq">Kawliya</a>. Around 95-98% of the country's 38 million citizens are <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-cia_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-2">[2]</a></sup> with small minorities of <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Iraq" title="Christianity in Iraq">Christians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yarsanism" title="Yarsanism">Yarsans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yazidism" title="Yazidism">Yezidis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaeans</a> also present. The official languages of Iraq are <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">Kurdish</a>. </p>
      <p>Iraq has a coastline measuring 58&nbsp;km (36 miles) on the northern <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and encompasses the <a href="/wiki/Tigris%E2%80%93Euphrates_river_system" title="Tigris–Euphrates river system">Mesopotamian Alluvial Plain</a>, the northwestern end of the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros mountain range</a> and the eastern part of the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Desert" title="Syrian Desert">Syrian Desert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Two major rivers, the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, run south through Iraq and into the <a href="/wiki/Shatt_al-Arab" title="Shatt al-Arab">Shatt al-Arab</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>. These rivers provide Iraq with significant amounts of fertile land. The region between the <a href="/wiki/Tigris%E2%80%93Euphrates_river_system" title="Tigris–Euphrates river system">Tigris and Euphrates rivers</a>, historically known as <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, is often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_civilization" title="Cradle of civilization">cradle of civilisation</a>. It was here that mankind first began to read, write, create laws and live in cities under an organised government—notably <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a>, from which "Iraq" is derived. The area has been home to successive civilisations since the <a href="/wiki/6th_millennium_BC" title="6th millennium BC">6th millennium BC</a>. Iraq was the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Third_Dynasty_of_Ur" title="Third Dynasty of Ur">Sumerian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Babylonian</a> empires. It was also part of the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Median</a>, <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Achaemenid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Hellenistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sassanid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid_dynasty" title="Ayyubid dynasty">Ayyubid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Mongol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safavid_Iran" title="Safavid Iran">Safavid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afsharid_dynasty" title="Afsharid dynasty">Afsharid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> empires.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The country today known as Iraq was a region of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Partition of the Ottoman Empire">partition of the Ottoman Empire</a> in the 20th century. It was made up of three provinces, called <a href="/wiki/Vilayet" title="Vilayet">vilayets</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Turkish language">Ottoman Turkish language</a>: <a href="/wiki/Mosul_Vilayet" title="Mosul Vilayet">Mosul Vilayet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baghdad_Vilayet" title="Baghdad Vilayet">Baghdad Vilayet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Basra_Vilayet" title="Basra Vilayet">Basra Vilayet</a>. In April 1920 the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_for_Mesopotamia" title="Mandate for Mesopotamia">British Mandate of Mesopotamia</a> was created under the authority of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>. A British-backed monarchy joining these <i>vilayets</i> into <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Iraq" title="Mandatory Iraq">one Kingdom</a> was established in 1921 under <a href="/wiki/Faisal_I_of_Iraq" title="Faisal I of Iraq">Faisal I of Iraq</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hashemites" title="Hashemites">Hashemite</a> <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">Kingdom of Iraq</a> gained independence from the UK in 1932. In 1958, <a href="/wiki/14_July_Revolution" title="14 July Revolution">the monarchy was overthrown</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Republic_(1958%E2%80%931968)" title="Iraqi Republic (1958–1968)">Iraqi Republic</a> created. Iraq was controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party_(Iraqi-dominated_faction)" title="Ba'ath Party (Iraqi-dominated faction)">Arab Socialist Ba'ath Party</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athist_Iraq" title="Ba'athist Iraq">1968 until 2003</a>. In 1980, Iraq invaded Iran, sparking <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">a protracted war which would last for almost eight years</a>, and end in a stalemate with devastating losses for both countries. </p>
      <p>After <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">an invasion</a> by the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Multi-National_Force_%E2%80%93_Iraq" title="Multi-National Force – Iraq">its allies</a> in 2003, <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a>'s Ba'ath Party was removed from power, and multi-party <a href="/wiki/December_2005_Iraqi_parliamentary_election" title="December 2005 Iraqi parliamentary election">parliamentary elections were held in 2005</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">US presence in Iraq</a> ended in 2011,<sup id="cite_ref-cnn-dec11_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-dec11-14">[14]</a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_insurgency_(2011%E2%80%932013)" title="Iraqi insurgency (2011–2013)">Iraqi insurgency</a> continued and intensified as fighters from the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war" title="Syrian civil war">Syrian civil war</a> spilled into the country. Out of the insurgency came a highly destructive group calling itself <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIL</a>, which took large parts of the north and west. It has since been largely defeated. Disputes over the sovereignty of <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Region" title="Kurdistan Region">Kurdistan Region</a> continue. A <a href="/wiki/2017_Kurdistan_Region_independence_referendum" title="2017 Kurdistan Region independence referendum">referendum</a> about the full sovereignty of Kurdistan Region was held on 25 September 2017. On 9 December 2017, then-<a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Iraq" title="Prime Minister of Iraq">Iraqi Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Haider_al-Abadi" title="Haider al-Abadi">Haider al-Abadi</a> declared victory over ISIL after the group lost <a href="/wiki/ISIL_territorial_claims" class="mw-redirect" title="ISIL territorial claims">its territory</a> in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Iraq is a <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federal</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a> consisting of <a href="/wiki/Governorates_of_Iraq" title="Governorates of Iraq">19 governorates</a>, four of which make up the autonomous <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Region" title="Kurdistan Region">Kurdistan Region</a>. The country's official religion is <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Iraq" title="Islam in Iraq">Islam</a>. <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iraq" title="Culture of Iraq">Culturally</a>, Iraq has a very rich heritage and celebrates the achievements of its past in both pre-Islamic as well as post-Islamic times and is known for its poets. Its painters and sculptors are among the best in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>, some of them being world-class as well as producing fine handicrafts, including rugs and <a href="/wiki/Carpet" title="Carpet">carpets</a>. Iraq is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> as well as of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">OIC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">IMF</a>. </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2> 
      <p><span class="anchor" id="Etymology"></span> The <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> name <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Arabic language) transliteration">al-ʿIrāq</i></i> (<span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic-language text">العراق</span>) has been in use since before the 6th century CE. </p>
      <p>There are several suggested origins for the name. One dates to the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a> (Biblical Hebrew <i>Erech</i>) and is thus ultimately of <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a> origin, as <i>Uruk</i> was the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> name for the Sumerian city of <i><i lang="sux-Latn" title="Sumerian-language text">Urug</i></i>, containing the Sumerian word for "city", <a href="/wiki/Cities_of_the_ancient_Near_East" class="mw-redirect" title="Cities of the ancient Near East">UR</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Another possible etymology for the name is from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a> word <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">erāq</span>, meaning "lowlands."<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> An “Aramaic <a href="/wiki/Incantation_bowl" title="Incantation bowl">incantation bowl</a>” excavated in <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a> features the word <i>’yrg</i> (<span lang="he" dir="rtl" title="Hebrew-language text">אירג</span>‎) next to <i>myšyn</i> (<a href="/wiki/Mesene" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesene">Mesene</a>) that suggests that it refers to the region of southern <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>An Arabic <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a> for the name is "deeply rooted, well-watered; <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">fertile</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the medieval period, there was a region called <i>ʿIrāq ʿArabī</i> ("Arabian Iraq") for Lower <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Persian_Iraq" title="Persian Iraq">ʿIrāq ʿAjamī</a></i> ("Persian Iraq"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworth1998538_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworth1998538-21">[21]</a></sup> for the region now situated in Central and Western Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworth1998538_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworth1998538-21">[21]</a></sup> The term historically included the plain south of the <a href="/wiki/Hamrin_Mountains" title="Hamrin Mountains">Hamrin Mountains</a> and did not include the northernmost and <a href="/wiki/Al-Jazira,_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Jazira, Mesopotamia">westernmost</a> parts of the modern territory of Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-Bernhardsson-97_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernhardsson-97-22">[22]</a></sup> Prior to the middle of the 19th century, the term <b>Eyraca Arabica</b> was commonly used to describe Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/Sawad" title="Sawad">Sawad</a></i> was also used in early Islamic times for the region of the <a href="/wiki/Alluvial_plain" title="Alluvial plain">alluvial plain</a> of the Tigris and Euphrates rivers, contrasting it with the arid Arabian desert. As an Arabic word, <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic-language text">عراق</span> means "hem", "shore", "bank", or "edge", so that the name by folk etymology came to be interpreted as "the <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">escarpment</a>", viz. at the south and east of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Jazira,_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Jazira, Mesopotamia">Jazira</a> Plateau, which forms the northern and western edge of the "al-Iraq arabi" area.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Arabic_phonology" title="Arabic phonology">Arabic pronunciation</a> is <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ʕiˈrɑːq]</a></span>. In English, it is either <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span> (the only pronunciation listed in the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> and the first one in <i>Merriam-Webster's Online Dictionary</i><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>) or <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span> (listed first by <a href="/wiki/Macquarie_Dictionary" title="Macquarie Dictionary"><i>MQD</i></a>), the <i>American Heritage Dictionary</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and the <i>Random House Dictionary</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The pronunciation <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span> is occasionally heard in US media.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In accordance with the 2005 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Iraq" title="Constitution of Iraq">Constitution</a>, the official name of the state is the <i>"Republic of Iraq"</i> (<i>Jumhūrīyyat al-'Irāq</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-1">[1]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iraq" title="History of Iraq">History of Iraq</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_era">Prehistoric era</span></h3> 
      <p>Between 65,000 BC and 35,000 BC, northern Iraq was home to a <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a> culture, archaeological remains of which have been discovered at <a href="/wiki/Shanidar_Cave" title="Shanidar Cave">Shanidar Cave</a><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> This same region is also the location of a number of pre-Neolithic cemeteries, dating from approximately 11,000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Ralph_S._Solecki_2004_pp._3_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ralph_S._Solecki_2004_pp._3-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since approximately 10,000 BC, Iraq, together with a large part of the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> also comprising <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Levant" class="mw-redirect" title="The Levant">the Levant</a>, was one of centres of a <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> culture known as <a href="/wiki/Pre-Pottery_Neolithic_A" title="Pre-Pottery Neolithic A">Pre-Pottery Neolithic A</a> (PPNA), where agriculture and cattle breeding appeared for the first time in the world. The following Neolithic period, <a href="/wiki/PPNB" class="mw-redirect" title="PPNB">PPNB</a>, is represented by rectangular houses. At the time of the pre-pottery Neolithic, people used vessels made of stone, <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> and burnt lime (Vaisselle blanche). Finds of <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">obsidian</a> tools from <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> are evidences of early trade relations. </p>
      <p>Further important sites of human advancement were <a href="/wiki/Jarmo" title="Jarmo">Jarmo</a> (circa 7100 BC),<sup id="cite_ref-Ralph_S._Solecki_2004_pp._3_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ralph_S._Solecki_2004_pp._3-30">[30]</a></sup> a number of sites belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Halaf_culture" title="Halaf culture">Halaf culture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tell_al-%27Ubaid" title="Tell al-'Ubaid">Tell al-'Ubaid</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Type_site" title="Type site">type site</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a> (between 6500 BC and 3800 BC).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The respective periods show ever-increasing levels of advancement in agriculture, tool-making and architecture. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Iraq">Ancient Iraq</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mesopotamia" title="History of Mesopotamia">History of Mesopotamia</a>
      </div>

         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="Question book-new.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399"></a>

          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cylinder_Seal,_Old_Babylonian,_formerly_in_the_Charterhouse_Collection_09.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cylinder_Seal%2C_Old_Babylonian%2C_formerly_in_the_Charterhouse_Collection_09.jpg/220px-Cylinder_Seal%2C_Old_Babylonian%2C_formerly_in_the_Charterhouse_Collection_09.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cylinder_Seal%2C_Old_Babylonian%2C_formerly_in_the_Charterhouse_Collection_09.jpg/330px-Cylinder_Seal%2C_Old_Babylonian%2C_formerly_in_the_Charterhouse_Collection_09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cylinder_Seal%2C_Old_Babylonian%2C_formerly_in_the_Charterhouse_Collection_09.jpg/440px-Cylinder_Seal%2C_Old_Babylonian%2C_formerly_in_the_Charterhouse_Collection_09.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1747" data-file-height="978"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cylinder_Seal,_Old_Babylonian,_formerly_in_the_Charterhouse_Collection_09.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Cylinder_Seal" class="mw-redirect" title="Cylinder Seal">Cylinder Seal</a>, Old Babylonian Period, c.1800 BC, <a href="/wiki/Hematite" title="Hematite">hematite</a>. The king makes an animal offering to <a href="/wiki/Shamash" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamash">Shamash</a>. This seal was probably made in a workshop at <a href="/wiki/Sippar" title="Sippar">Sippar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The historical period in Iraq truly begins during the <a href="/wiki/Uruk_period" title="Uruk period">Uruk period</a> (4000 BC to 3100 BC), with the founding of a number of <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Cities">cities</a>, and the use of <a href="/wiki/Pictographs" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictographs">Pictographs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_seals" class="mw-redirect" title="Cylinder seals">Cylinder seals</a> and mass-produced goods.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The "<a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_Civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Cradle of Civilization">Cradle of Civilization</a>" is thus a common term for the area comprising modern Iraq as it was home to the earliest known <a href="/wiki/Civilisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Civilisation">civilisation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian civilisation</a>, which arose in the fertile <a href="/wiki/Tigris-Euphrates_river_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Tigris-Euphrates river system">Tigris-Euphrates river valley</a> of southern Iraq in the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a>). </p>
      <p>It was here, in the late <a href="/wiki/4th_millennium_BC" title="4th millennium BC">4th millennium BC</a>, that the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_first_written_accounts" title="List of languages by first written accounts">first</a> <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuneiform script">writing system</a> and recorded history itself were born. The <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> were also the first to harness the <a href="/wiki/Wheel" title="Wheel">wheel</a> and create <a href="/wiki/City_States" class="mw-redirect" title="City States">City States</a>, and whose writings record the first evidence of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">Mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">Astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">Astrology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Written_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Written Law">Written Law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">Medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organised_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Organised religion">Organised religion</a>. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">language</a> of the Sumerians is a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>. The major city states of the early Sumerian period were; <a href="/wiki/Eridu" title="Eridu">Eridu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bad-tibira" title="Bad-tibira">Bad-tibira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sippar" title="Sippar">Sippar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shuruppak" title="Shuruppak">Shuruppak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Girsu" title="Girsu">Girsu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umma" title="Umma">Umma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamazi" title="Hamazi">Hamazi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adab_(city)" title="Adab (city)">Adab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria" title="Mari, Syria">Mari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kutha" title="Kutha">Kutha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Der_(Sumer)" title="Der (Sumer)">Der</a> and <a href="/wiki/Akshak" title="Akshak">Akshak</a>. </p>
      <p>The cities to the north like <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">Ashur</a>, Arbela (modern <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Erbil</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Arrapha" title="Arrapha">Arrapha</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a>) were also extant in what was to be called Assyria from the 25th century BC; however, at this early stage, they were Sumerian ruled administrative centres. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:P1150890_Louvre_st%C3%A8le_de_victoire_Akkad_AO2678_rwk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/P1150890_Louvre_st%C3%A8le_de_victoire_Akkad_AO2678_rwk.jpg/220px-P1150890_Louvre_st%C3%A8le_de_victoire_Akkad_AO2678_rwk.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/P1150890_Louvre_st%C3%A8le_de_victoire_Akkad_AO2678_rwk.jpg/330px-P1150890_Louvre_st%C3%A8le_de_victoire_Akkad_AO2678_rwk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/P1150890_Louvre_st%C3%A8le_de_victoire_Akkad_AO2678_rwk.jpg/440px-P1150890_Louvre_st%C3%A8le_de_victoire_Akkad_AO2678_rwk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1873"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:P1150890_Louvre_st%C3%A8le_de_victoire_Akkad_AO2678_rwk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Victory stele of <a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Akkad" title="Naram-Sin of Akkad">Naram-Sin of Akkad</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age">Bronze Age</span></h4> 
      <p>In the 26th century BC, <a href="/wiki/Eannatum" title="Eannatum">Eannatum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a> created what was perhaps the first <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">empire</a> in history, though this was short-lived. Later, <a href="/wiki/Lugal-Zage-Si" class="mw-redirect" title="Lugal-Zage-Si">Lugal-Zage-Si</a>, the priest-king of <a href="/wiki/Umma" title="Umma">Umma</a>, overthrew the primacy of the Lagash dynasty in the area, then conquered <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a>, making it his capital, and claimed an empire extending from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> It was during this period that the <a href="/wiki/Epic_of_Gilgamesh" title="Epic of Gilgamesh">Epic of Gilgamesh</a> originates, which includes the tale of <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Flood" class="mw-redirect" title="The Great Flood">The Great Flood</a>. </p>
      <p>From the 29th century BC, Akkadian Semitic names began to appear on king lists and administrative documents of various city states. It remains unknown as to the origin of Akkad, where it was precisely situated and how it rose to prominence. Its people spoke <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a>, an <a href="/wiki/East_Semitic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="East Semitic language">East Semitic language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the 3rd millennium BC, a cultural symbiosis developed between the Sumerians and the Akkadians, which included widespread <a href="/wiki/Bilingualism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilingualism">bilingualism</a>. The influences between <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> are evident in all areas, including lexical borrowing on a massive scale—and syntactic, morphological, and phonological convergence. This mutual influence has prompted scholars to refer to Sumerian and Akkadian of the 3rd millennium BC as a <a href="/wiki/Sprachbund" title="Sprachbund">Sprachbund</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> From this period, the civilisation in Iraq came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Sumero-Akkadian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sumero-Akkadian">Sumero-Akkadian</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bill_of_sale_Louvre_AO3765.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Bill_of_sale_Louvre_AO3765.jpg/170px-Bill_of_sale_Louvre_AO3765.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Bill_of_sale_Louvre_AO3765.jpg/255px-Bill_of_sale_Louvre_AO3765.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Bill_of_sale_Louvre_AO3765.jpg/340px-Bill_of_sale_Louvre_AO3765.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1650" data-file-height="1600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bill_of_sale_Louvre_AO3765.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Bill of sale of a male slave and a building in <a href="/wiki/Shuruppak" title="Shuruppak">Shuruppak</a>, Sumerian tablet, circa 2600 BC.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Between the 29th and 24th centuries BC, a number of kingdoms and city states within Iraq began to have Akkadian speaking dynasties; including <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ekallatum" title="Ekallatum">Ekallatum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a>. </p>
      <p>However, the Sumerians remained generally dominant until the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> (2335–2124 BC), based in the city of <a href="/wiki/Akkad_(city)" title="Akkad (city)">Akkad</a> in central Iraq. <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a>, originally a <a href="/wiki/Rabshakeh" title="Rabshakeh">Rabshakeh</a> to a Sumerian king, founded the empire, he conquered all of the city states of southern and central Iraq, and subjugated the kings of Assyria, thus uniting the Sumerians and Akkadians in one state. He then set about expanding his empire, conquering <a href="/wiki/Gutium" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutium">Gutium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> and had victories that did not result into a full conquest against the <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Eblaites</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Syria">Ancient Syria</a>. </p>
      <p>After the collapse of the Akkadian Empire in the late 22nd century BC, the <a href="/wiki/Gutians" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutians">Gutians</a> occupied the south for a few decades, while Assyria reasserted its independence in the north. This was followed by a Sumerian renaissance in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Sumerian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Sumerian Empire">Neo-Sumerian Empire</a>. The Sumerians under king <a href="/wiki/Shulgi" title="Shulgi">Shulgi</a> conquered almost all of Iraq except the northern reaches of Assyria, and asserted themselves over the <a href="/wiki/Gutians" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutians">Gutians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elamites" class="mw-redirect" title="Elamites">Elamites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorites</a>, destroying the first and holding off the others. </p>
      <p>An <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elamite</a> invasion in 2004 BC brought the Sumerian revival to an end. By the mid 21st century BC, the Akkadian speaking kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> had risen to dominance in northern Iraq. Assyria expanded territorially into the north eastern Levant, central Iraq, and eastern Anatolia, forming the <a href="/wiki/Old_Assyrian_Empire" title="Old Assyrian Empire">Old Assyrian Empire</a> (circa 2035–1750 BC) under kings such as <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Ashur_I" title="Puzur-Ashur I">Puzur-Ashur I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sargon_I" title="Sargon I">Sargon I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ilushuma" class="mw-redirect" title="Ilushuma">Ilushuma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erishum_I" title="Erishum I">Erishum I</a>, the latter of whom produced the most detailed set of law yet written.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The south broke up into a number of Akkadian speaking states, <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">Eshnunna</a> being the major ones. </p>
      <p>During the 20th century BC, the <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Canaanite language">Canaanite</a> speaking <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorites</a> began to migrate into southern Mesopotamia. Eventually, they began to set up small petty kingdoms in the south, as well as usurping the thrones of extant city states such as <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">Eshnunna</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg/170px-F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg/255px-F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg/340px-F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3170" data-file-height="3500"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:F0182_Louvre_Code_Hammourabi_Bas-relief_Sb8_rwk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a>, depicted as receiving his royal insignia from <a href="/wiki/Shamash" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamash">Shamash</a>. Relief on the upper part of the stele of <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Hammurabi's code of laws</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>One of these small Amorite kingdoms founded in 1894 BC contained the then small administrative town of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> within its borders. It remained insignificant for over a century, overshadowed by older and more powerful states, such as Assyria, Elam, Isin, Ehnunna and Larsa. </p>
      <p>In 1792 BC, an <a href="/wiki/Amorite" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorite">Amorite</a> ruler named <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a> came to power in this state, and immediately set about building Babylon from a minor town into a major city, declaring himself its king. Hammurabi conquered the whole of southern and central Iraq, as well as Elam to the east and Mari to the west, then engaged in a protracted war with the Assyrian king <a href="/wiki/Ishme-Dagan" title="Ishme-Dagan">Ishme-Dagan</a> for domination of the region, creating the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Babylonian Empire">Babylonian Empire</a>. He eventually prevailed over the successor of Ishme-Dagan and subjected Assyria and its Anatolian colonies. By the middle of the eighteenth century BC, the Sumerians had lost their cultural identity and ceased to exist as a distinct people.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Genetic and cultural analysis indicates that the <a href="/wiki/Marsh_Arabs" title="Marsh Arabs">Marsh Arabs</a> of southern Iraq are probably their most direct modern descendants.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>It is from the period of Hammurabi that southern Iraq came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, while the north had already coalesced into <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> hundreds of years before. However, his empire was short-lived, and rapidly collapsed after his death, with both Assyria and southern Iraq, in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Sealand_Dynasty" title="Sealand Dynasty">Sealand Dynasty</a>, falling back into native Akkadian hands. The foreign Amorites clung on to power in a once more weak and small Babylonia until it was sacked by the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> speaking <a href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a> based in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> in 1595 BC. After this, another foreign people, the <a href="/wiki/Language_Isolate" class="mw-redirect" title="Language Isolate">Language Isolate</a> speaking <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a>, originating in the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Iran">Ancient Iran</a>, seized control of Babylonia, where they were to rule for almost 600 years, by far the longest dynasty ever to rule in Babylon. </p>
      <p>Iraq was from this point divided into three polities: <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> in the north, <a href="/wiki/Kassite" class="mw-redirect" title="Kassite">Kassite</a> <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a> in the south central region, and the <a href="/wiki/Sealand_Dynasty" title="Sealand Dynasty">Sealand Dynasty</a> in the far south. The Sealand Dynasty was finally conquered by Kassite Babylonia circa 1380 BC. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Middle Assyrian Empire</a> (1365–1020 BC) saw Assyria rise to be the most powerful nation in the known world. Beginning with the campaigns of <a href="/wiki/Ashur-uballit_I" title="Ashur-uballit I">Ashur-uballit I</a>, Assyria destroyed the rival <a href="/wiki/Hurrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurrian">Hurrian</a>-<a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">Mitanni</a> Empire, annexed huge swathes of the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a> for itself, annexed northern Babylonia from the Kassites, forced the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Empire">Egyptian Empire</a> from the region, and defeated the <a href="/wiki/Elamites" class="mw-redirect" title="Elamites">Elamites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canaanites" class="mw-redirect" title="Canaanites">Canaanites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cilicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Cilicians">Cilicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gutians" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutians">Gutians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dilmun" title="Dilmun">Dilmunites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a>. At its height, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Middle Assyrian Empire</a> stretched from <a href="/wiki/The_Caucasus" class="mw-redirect" title="The Caucasus">The Caucasus</a> to <a href="/wiki/Dilmun" title="Dilmun">Dilmun</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>), and from the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> coasts of <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>. In 1235 BC, <a href="/wiki/Tukulti-Ninurta_I" title="Tukulti-Ninurta I">Tukulti-Ninurta I</a> of Assyria took the throne of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, thus becoming the first <i>native Mesopotamian</i> to rule the state. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Jehu-Obelisk-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Jehu-Obelisk-cropped.jpg/220px-Jehu-Obelisk-cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Jehu-Obelisk-cropped.jpg/330px-Jehu-Obelisk-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Jehu-Obelisk-cropped.jpg/440px-Jehu-Obelisk-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1557" data-file-height="999"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Jehu-Obelisk-cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Jehu" title="Jehu">Jehu</a>, king of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Israel</a>, bows before Shalmaneser III of <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, 825 BC.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronze Age collapse">Bronze Age collapse</a> (1200–900 BC), Babylonia was in a state of chaos, dominated for long periods by Assyria and <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a>. The Kassites were driven from power by Assyria and Elam, allowing native south Mesopotamian kings to rule Babylonia for the first time, although often subject to Assyrian or Elamite rulers. However, these <a href="/wiki/East_Semitic_languages" title="East Semitic languages">East Semitic</a> Akkadian kings, were unable to prevent new waves of <a href="/wiki/West_Semitic_languages" title="West Semitic languages">West Semitic</a> migrants entering southern Iraq, and during the 11th century BC <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suteans" title="Suteans">Suteans</a> entered Babylonia from <a href="/wiki/The_Levant" class="mw-redirect" title="The Levant">The Levant</a>, and these were followed in the late 10th to early 9th century BC by the migrant <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a> who were closely related to the earlier <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a>. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iron_Age">Iron Age</span></h4> 
      <p>After a period of comparative decline in Assyria, it once more began to expand with the <a href="/wiki/Neo_Assyrian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo Assyrian Empire">Neo Assyrian Empire</a> (935–605 BC). This was to be the largest empire the region had yet seen, and under rulers such as <a href="/wiki/Adad-Nirari_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Adad-Nirari II">Adad-Nirari II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ashurnasirpal_I" title="Ashurnasirpal I">Ashurnasirpal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_III" title="Shalmaneser III">Shalmaneser III</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semiramis" title="Semiramis">Semiramis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tiglath-pileser_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiglath-pileser III">Tiglath-pileser III</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sargon_II" title="Sargon II">Sargon II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib" title="Sennacherib">Sennacherib</a>, <a href="/wiki/Esarhaddon" title="Esarhaddon">Esarhaddon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">Ashurbanipal</a>, Iraq became the centre of an empire stretching from <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> in the east, to <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> in the west, and from <a href="/wiki/The_Caucasus" class="mw-redirect" title="The Caucasus">The Caucasus</a> in the north to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> in the south. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Arab_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab people">Arabs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a> are first mentioned in written history (circa 850 BC) in the annals of <a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_III" title="Shalmaneser III">Shalmaneser III</a>. </p>
      <p>It was during this period that an Akkadian influenced form of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Aramaic">Eastern Aramaic</a> was adopted by the Assyrians as the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of their vast empire, and Mesopotamian Aramaic began to supplant Akkadian as the spoken language of the general populace of both Assyria and Babylonia. The descendant dialects of this tongue survive amongst the <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Mandaeans</a> of southern Iraq and <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> of northern Iraq to this day. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Loewenjagd_-645-635_Niniveh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Loewenjagd_-645-635_Niniveh.JPG/220px-Loewenjagd_-645-635_Niniveh.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Loewenjagd_-645-635_Niniveh.JPG/330px-Loewenjagd_-645-635_Niniveh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Loewenjagd_-645-635_Niniveh.JPG/440px-Loewenjagd_-645-635_Niniveh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Loewenjagd_-645-635_Niniveh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Relief showing a <a href="/wiki/Lion_hunting" title="Lion hunting">lion hunt</a>, from the north palace of <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>, 645–635 BC.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the late 7th century BC, the Assyrian Empire tore itself apart with a series of brutal civil wars, weakening itself to such a degree that a coalition of its former subjects; the <a href="/wiki/Babylonians" class="mw-redirect" title="Babylonians">Babylonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian people">Persians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a>, were able to attack Assyria, finally bringing its empire down by 605 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Babylonian_and_Persian_periods">Babylonian and Persian periods</span></h4> 
      <p>The short-lived <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a> (620–539 BC) succeeded that of Assyria. It failed to attain the size, power or longevity of its predecessor; however, it came to dominate <a href="/wiki/The_Levant" class="mw-redirect" title="The Levant">The Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Judah</a>, and to defeat <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. Initially, Babylon was ruled by yet another foreign dynasty, that of the <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a>, who had migrated to the region in the late 10th or early 9th century BC. Its greatest king, <a href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar_II" title="Nebuchadnezzar II">Nebuchadnezzar II</a>, rivalled another non native ruler, the ethnically unrelated <a href="/wiki/Amorite" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorite">Amorite</a> king <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a>, as the greatest king of Babylon. However, by 556 BC, the Chaldeans had been deposed from power by the Assyrian born <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">Nabonidus</a> and his son and regent <a href="/wiki/Belshazzar" title="Belshazzar">Belshazzar</a>. </p>
      <p>In the 6th century BC, <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> of neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persia</a> defeated the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Neo-Babylonian Empire</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Opis" title="Battle of Opis">Battle of Opis</a> and Iraq was subsumed into the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> for nearly two centuries. The Achaemenids made <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> their main capital. The Chaldeans and Chaldea disappeared at around this time, though both Assyria and Babylonia endured and thrived under Achaemenid rule (see <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Achaemenid Assyria</a>). Little changed under the Persians, having spent three centuries under Assyrian rule, their kings saw themselves as successors to Ashurbanipal, and they retained Assyrian Imperial Aramaic as the language of empire, together with the Assyrian imperial infrastructure, and an Assyrian style of art and architecture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/220px-Diadochen1.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="106" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/330px-Diadochen1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Diadochen1.png/440px-Diadochen1.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="961"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Diadochen1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Greek-ruled <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> (in yellow) with capital in <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> on the Tigris, north of Babylon.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the late 4th century BC, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> conquered the region, putting it under <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid</a> rule for over two centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The Seleucids introduced the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Anatolian" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Anatolian">Indo-Anatolian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> term <i>Syria</i> to the region. This name had for many centuries been the Indo-European word for <i>Assyria</i> and specifically and only meant Assyria; however, the Seleucids also applied it to <a href="/wiki/The_Levant" class="mw-redirect" title="The Levant">The Levant</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aramea" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramea">Aramea</a>, causing both the Assyria and the Assyrians of Iraq and the <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a> and The Levant to be called Syria and Syrians/Syriacs in the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco-Roman">Greco-Roman</a> world.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hatra_ruins.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Hatra_ruins.jpg/220px-Hatra_ruins.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Hatra_ruins.jpg/330px-Hatra_ruins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Hatra_ruins.jpg/440px-Hatra_ruins.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="396"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hatra_ruins.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Flourished in the 2nd century, the strongly fortified Parthian city of <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a> shows a unique blend of both <a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">Classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_architecture" title="Iranian architecture">Persian</a> architecture and art.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> (247 BC – 224 AD) from Persia conquered the region during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_I_of_Parthia" title="Mithridates I of Parthia">Mithridates I of Parthia</a> (r. 171–138 BC). From <a href="/wiki/Syria_(Roman_province)" class="mw-redirect" title="Syria (Roman province)">Syria</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a> invaded western parts of the region <a href="/wiki/Roman-Parthian_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman-Parthian Wars">several times</a>, briefly founding <i>Assyria Provincia</i> in Assyria. <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> began to take hold in Iraq (particularly in <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>) between the 1st and 3rd centuries, and Assyria became a centre of <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_literature" title="Syriac literature">Syriac literature</a>. A number of independent states evolved in the north during the Parthian era, such as <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a>. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid dynasty">Sassanids</a> of Persia under <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir I</a> destroyed the Parthian Empire and conquered the region in 224 AD. During the 240s and 250's AD, the Sassanids gradually conquered the independent states, culminating with Assur in 256 AD. The region was thus a province of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empire</a> for over four centuries, and became the frontier and battle ground between the Sassanid Empire and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, with both empires weakening each other, paving the way for the <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a>-<a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Muslim conquest of Persia</a> in the mid-7th century. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Abbasids850.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Abbasids850.png/300px-Abbasids850.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Abbasids850.png/450px-Abbasids850.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Abbasids850.png/600px-Abbasids850.png 2x" data-file-width="1481" data-file-height="902"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Abbasids850.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> at its greatest extent, c. 850.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Arab Islamic conquest in the mid-7th century AD established <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> in Iraq and saw a large influx of <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>. Under the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>, the prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>'s cousin and son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a>, moved his capital to <a href="/wiki/Kufa" title="Kufa">Kufa</a> when he became the fourth <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">caliph</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> ruled the province of Iraq from <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> in the 7th century. (However, eventually there was a separate, independent <a href="/wiki/Caliphate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Caliphate of Córdoba">Caliphate of Córdoba</a> in Iberia.) </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> built the city of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> along the Tigris in the 8th century as its capital, and the city became the leading metropolis of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> for five centuries. Baghdad was the largest <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multicultural</a> <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">city</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, peaking at a population of more than a million,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and was the centre of learning during the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongols</a> destroyed the city and burned its library during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">siege of Baghdad</a> in the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1257, <a href="/wiki/Hulagu_Khan" title="Hulagu Khan">Hulagu Khan</a> amassed an unusually large army, a significant portion of the Mongol Empire's forces, for the purpose of conquering Baghdad. When they arrived at the Islamic capital, Hulagu Khan demanded its surrender, but the last Abbasid Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Musta%27sim" title="Al-Musta'sim">Al-Musta'sim</a> refused. This angered Hulagu, and, consistent with Mongol strategy of discouraging resistance, he <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">besieged Baghdad</a>, sacked the city and massacred many of the inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Estimates of the number of dead range from 200,000 to a million.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bagdad1258.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bagdad1258.jpg/220px-Bagdad1258.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bagdad1258.jpg/330px-Bagdad1258.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bagdad1258.jpg/440px-Bagdad1258.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2074" data-file-height="1464"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bagdad1258.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">sack of Baghdad</a> by the Mongols.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Mongols destroyed the Abbasid Caliphate and Baghdad's <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a>, which contained countless precious and historical documents. The city has never regained its previous pre-eminence as a major centre of culture and influence. Some historians believe that the Mongol invasion destroyed much of the <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> infrastructure that had sustained Mesopotamia for millennia. Other historians point to <a href="/wiki/Soil_salination" class="mw-redirect" title="Soil salination">soil salination</a> as the culprit in the decline in agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The mid-14th-century <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> ravaged much of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The best estimate for the Middle East is a death rate of roughly one-third.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1401, a warlord of Mongol descent, <a href="/wiki/Tamerlane" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamerlane">Tamerlane</a> (Timur Lenk), invaded Iraq. After the capture of Baghdad, 20,000 of its citizens were massacred.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Timur ordered that every soldier should return with at least two severed human heads to show him (many warriors were so scared they killed prisoners captured earlier in the campaign just to ensure they had heads to present to Timur).<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Timur also conducted massacres of the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> population, hitherto still the majority population in northern Mesopotamia, and it was during this time that the ancient Assyrian city of <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">Assur</a> was finally abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Iraq">Ottoman Iraq</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Iraq" title="Ottoman Iraq">Ottoman Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_dynasty_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Mamluk dynasty of Iraq">Mamluk dynasty of Iraq</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cedid_Atlas_(Middle_East)_1803.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Cedid_Atlas_%28Middle_East%29_1803.jpg/350px-Cedid_Atlas_%28Middle_East%29_1803.jpg" decoding="async" width="350" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Cedid_Atlas_%28Middle_East%29_1803.jpg/525px-Cedid_Atlas_%28Middle_East%29_1803.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Cedid_Atlas_%28Middle_East%29_1803.jpg/700px-Cedid_Atlas_%28Middle_East%29_1803.jpg 2x" data-file-width="10840" data-file-height="8199"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cedid_Atlas_(Middle_East)_1803.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The 1803 <a href="/wiki/Cedid_Atlas" title="Cedid Atlas">Cedid Atlas</a>, showing the area today known as Iraq divided between "<a href="/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia" title="Upper Mesopotamia">Al Jazira</a>" (pink), "<a href="/wiki/Kurdistan" title="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a>" (blue), "Iraq" (green), and "<a href="/wiki/Al_Sham" class="mw-redirect" title="Al Sham">Al Sham</a>" (yellow).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During the late 14th and early 15th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sheep_Turkmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Sheep Turkmen">Black Sheep Turkmen</a> ruled the area now known as Iraq. In 1466, the <a href="/wiki/White_Sheep_Turkmen" class="mw-redirect" title="White Sheep Turkmen">White Sheep Turkmen</a> defeated the Black Sheep and took control. From the earliest 16th century, in 1508, as with all territories of the former White Sheep Turkmen, Iraq fell into the hands of the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Safavids" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavids">Safavids</a>. Owing to the century long Turco-Iranian rivalry between the Safavids and the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottoman Turks</a>, Iraq would be contested between the two for more than a hundred years during the frequent <a href="/wiki/Ottoman-Persian_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman-Persian Wars">Ottoman-Persian Wars</a>. </p>
      <p>With the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zuhab" title="Treaty of Zuhab">Treaty of Zuhab</a> in 1639, most of the territory of present-day Iraq eventually came under the control of Ottoman Empire as the <a href="/wiki/Eyalet_of_Baghdad" class="mw-redirect" title="Eyalet of Baghdad">eyalet of Baghdad</a> as a result of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman-Persian_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman-Persian Wars">wars</a> with the neighbouring rival, <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid Iran</a>. Throughout most of the period of Ottoman rule (1533–1918), the territory of present-day Iraq was a battle zone between the rival regional empires and tribal alliances. </p>
      <p>By the 17th century, the frequent conflicts with the Safavids had sapped the strength of the Ottoman Empire and had weakened its control over its provinces. The nomadic population swelled with the influx of <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">bedouins</a> from <a href="/wiki/Najd" title="Najd">Najd</a>, in the Arabian Peninsula. Bedouin raids on settled areas became impossible to curb.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Layard_Nineveh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Layard_Nineveh.jpg/220px-Layard_Nineveh.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Layard_Nineveh.jpg/330px-Layard_Nineveh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Layard_Nineveh.jpg/440px-Layard_Nineveh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="598" data-file-height="360"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Layard_Nineveh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>English archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Austen_Henry_Layard" title="Austen Henry Layard">Austen Henry Layard</a> in the ancient <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>, 1852.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During the years 1747–1831, Iraq was ruled by a <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_dynasty_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Mamluk dynasty of Iraq">Mamluk dynasty</a> of <a href="/wiki/Georgian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Georgian people">Georgian</a><sup id="cite_ref-bbs_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbs-58">[58]</a></sup> origin who succeeded in obtaining autonomy from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Porte" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Porte">Ottoman Porte</a>, suppressed tribal revolts, curbed the power of the Janissaries, restored order and introduced a programme of modernisation of economy and military. In 1831, the Ottomans managed to overthrow the Mamluk regime and imposed their direct control over Iraq. The population of Iraq, estimated at 30 million in 800 AD, was only 5 million at the start of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the Ottomans sided with <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_campaign" title="Mesopotamian campaign">Mesopotamian campaign</a> against the Central Powers, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> forces invaded the country and initially suffered a major defeat at the hands of the Turkish army during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Kut" title="Siege of Kut">Siege of Kut</a> (1915–1916). However, subsequent to this the British began to gain the upper hand, and were further aided by the support of local <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Iraq" title="Assyrians in Iraq">Assyrians</a>. In 1916, the British and French made a plan for the post-war division of <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Sykes-Picot_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Sykes-Picot Agreement">Sykes-Picot Agreement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> British forces regrouped and <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Baghdad_(1917)" title="Fall of Baghdad (1917)">captured Baghdad</a> in 1917, and defeated the Ottomans. An armistice was signed in 1918. The British lost 92,000 soldiers in the Mesopotamian campaign. Ottoman losses are unknown but the British captured a total of 45,000 <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">prisoners of war</a>. By the end of 1918, the British had deployed 410,000 men in the area, of which 112,000 were combat troops.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_period">Contemporary period</span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="British_administration_and_independent_kingdom">British administration and independent kingdom</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Iraq" title="Mandatory Iraq">Mandatory Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">Kingdom of Iraq</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:BritsLookingOnBaghdad1941.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/BritsLookingOnBaghdad1941.jpg/170px-BritsLookingOnBaghdad1941.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/BritsLookingOnBaghdad1941.jpg/255px-BritsLookingOnBaghdad1941.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/BritsLookingOnBaghdad1941.jpg/340px-BritsLookingOnBaghdad1941.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="798"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:BritsLookingOnBaghdad1941.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>British troops in Baghdad, June 1941.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The country today known as Iraq was a region of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Partition of the Ottoman Empire">partition of the Ottoman Empire</a> in the 20th century. It was made up of three provinces, called <a href="/wiki/Vilayet" title="Vilayet">vilayets</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman language">Ottoman language</a>: <a href="/wiki/Mosul_Vilayet" title="Mosul Vilayet">Mosul Vilayet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baghdad_Vilayet" title="Baghdad Vilayet">Baghdad Vilayet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Basra_Vilayet" title="Basra Vilayet">Basra Vilayet</a>. These three provinces were joined into one Kingdom by the British after the region became a <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">League of Nations mandate</a>, administered under British control, with the name "<a href="/wiki/British_Mandate_of_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="British Mandate of Mesopotamia">State of Iraq</a>".
