A fourth province (<a href="/wiki/Zor_Sanjak" title="Zor Sanjak">Zor Sanjak</a>), which <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_nationalism" title="Iraqi nationalism">Iraqi nationalists</a> considered part of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia" title="Upper Mesopotamia">Upper Mesopotamia</a> was ultimately <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Zor" title="Occupation of Zor">added to Syria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In line with their "<a href="/wiki/Sharifian_Solution" title="Sharifian Solution">Sharifian Solution</a>" policy, the British established the <a href="/wiki/Hashemites" title="Hashemites">Hashemite</a> king, <a href="/wiki/Faisal_I_of_Iraq" title="Faisal I of Iraq">Faisal I of Iraq</a>, who had been forced out of <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> by the French, as their client ruler. Likewise, British authorities selected <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> Arab elites from the region for appointments to government and ministry offices.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (April 2007)">specify</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2013)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Faced with spiraling costs and influenced by the public protestations of the war hero <a href="/wiki/T._E._Lawrence" title="T. E. Lawrence">T. E. Lawrence</a><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>, Britain replaced <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Wilson" title="Arnold Wilson">Arnold Wilson</a> in October 1920 with a new Civil Commissioner, <a href="/wiki/Percy_Zachariah_Cox" class="mw-redirect" title="Percy Zachariah Cox">Sir Percy Cox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Cox managed to quell a rebellion, yet was also responsible for implementing the fateful policy of close co-operation with Iraq's Sunni minority.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The institution of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> was abolished in the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-zanj_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zanj-68">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Britain granted independence to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">Kingdom of Iraq</a> in 1932,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> on the urging of <a href="/wiki/Faisal_I_of_Iraq" title="Faisal I of Iraq">King Faisal</a>, though the British retained <a href="/wiki/Military_base" title="Military base">military bases</a>, local militia in the form of <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Levies" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian Levies">Assyrian Levies</a>, and transit rights for their forces. King <a href="/wiki/Ghazi_of_Iraq" title="Ghazi of Iraq">Ghazi</a> ruled as a figurehead after King Faisal's death in 1933, while undermined by attempted military <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coups</a>, until his death in 1939. Ghazi was followed by his underage son, <a href="/wiki/Faisal_II_of_Iraq" title="Faisal II of Iraq">Faisal II</a>. <a href="/wiki/%27Abd_al-Ilah" title="'Abd al-Ilah">'Abd al-Ilah</a> served as <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">Regent</a> during Faisal's minority. </p>
      <p>On 1 April 1941, <a href="/wiki/Rashid_Ali_al-Gaylani" title="Rashid Ali al-Gaylani">Rashid Ali al-Gaylani</a> and members of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Square_(Iraq)" title="Golden Square (Iraq)">Golden Square</a> staged a <a href="/wiki/1941_Iraqi_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1941 Iraqi coup d'état">coup d'état and overthrew the government of 'Abd al-Ilah</a>. During the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Iraqi_War" title="Anglo-Iraqi War">Anglo-Iraqi War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (which still maintained air bases in Iraq) invaded Iraq for fear that the Rashid Ali government might cut oil supplies to Western nations because of his links to the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a>. The war started on 2 May, and the British, together with loyal <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Levies" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian Levies">Assyrian Levies</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> defeated the forces of Al-Gaylani, forcing an armistice on 31 May. </p>
      <p>A <a href="/wiki/Military_occupation" title="Military occupation">military occupation</a> followed the restoration of the pre-coup government of the <a href="/wiki/Hashemites" title="Hashemites">Hashemite</a> monarchy. The occupation ended on 26 October 1947, although Britain was to retain military bases in Iraq until 1954, after which the Assyrian militias were disbanded. The rulers during the occupation and the remainder of the Hashemite monarchy were <a href="/wiki/Nuri_as-Said" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuri as-Said">Nuri as-Said</a>, the autocratic Prime Minister, who also ruled from 1930 to 1932, and 'Abd al-Ilah, the former Regent who now served as an adviser to King Faisal II. </p> 
      <h4><span id="Republic_and_Ba.27athist_Iraq"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Republic_and_Ba'athist_Iraq">Republic and Ba'athist Iraq</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Republic_(1958%E2%80%9368)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Republic (1958–68)">Iraqi Republic (1958–68)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athist_Iraq" title="Ba'athist Iraq">Ba'athist Iraq</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:1958_revolution_in_Iraq.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/1958_revolution_in_Iraq.jpg/220px-1958_revolution_in_Iraq.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/1958_revolution_in_Iraq.jpg/330px-1958_revolution_in_Iraq.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/1958_revolution_in_Iraq.jpg/440px-1958_revolution_in_Iraq.jpg 2x" data-file-width="531" data-file-height="411"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:1958_revolution_in_Iraq.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/14_July_Revolution" title="14 July Revolution">14 July Revolution</a> in 1958.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1958, a coup d'état known as the <a href="/wiki/14_July_Revolution" title="14 July Revolution">14 July Revolution</a> was led by the Brigadier General <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Karim_Qasim" title="Abd al-Karim Qasim">Abd al-Karim Qasim</a>. This revolt was strongly anti-imperial and anti-monarchical in nature and had strong socialist elements. Numerous people were killed in the coup, including King <a href="/wiki/Faysal_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Faysal II">Faysal II</a>, Prince <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Ilah" class="mw-redirect" title="Abd al-Ilah">Abd al-Ilah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuri_al-Sa%27id" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuri al-Sa'id">Nuri al-Sa'id</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Qasim controlled Iraq through military rule and in 1958 he began a process of forcibly reducing the surplus amounts of land owned by a few citizens and having the state redistribute the land. He was overthrown by Colonel <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Salam_Arif" title="Abdul Salam Arif">Abdul Salam Arif</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Ramadan_Revolution" title="Ramadan Revolution">February 1963 coup</a>. After the latter's death in 1966, he was succeeded by his brother, <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rahman_Arif" title="Abdul Rahman Arif">Abdul Rahman Arif</a>, who was <a href="/wiki/17_July_Revolution" title="17 July Revolution">overthrown</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Socialist_Ba%27ath_Party_%E2%80%93_Iraq_Region" title="Arab Socialist Ba'ath Party – Iraq Region">Ba'ath Party</a> in 1968. <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Hassan_al-Bakr" title="Ahmed Hassan al-Bakr">Ahmed Hassan al-Bakr</a> became the first Ba'ath <a href="/wiki/President_of_Iraq" title="President of Iraq">President of Iraq</a> but then the movement gradually came under the control of <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a>, who acceded to the presidency and control of the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Revolutionary_Command_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Revolutionary Command Council">Revolutionary Command Council</a> (RCC), then Iraq's supreme executive body, in July 1979. </p>
      <p>In 1979, the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Iranian Revolution</a> took place. Following months of cross-border raids between the two countries, Saddam declared war on Iran in September 1980, initiating the <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a> (or First Persian Gulf War). Taking advantage of the post-revolution chaos in Iran, Iraq captured some territories in southwest of Iran, but Iran recaptured all of the lost territories within two years, and for the next six years Iran was on the offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2014)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The war, which ended in <a href="/wiki/Stalemate" title="Stalemate">stalemate</a> in 1988, had cost the lives of between half a million and 1.5 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In 1981, Israeli aircraft <a href="/wiki/Operation_Opera" title="Operation Opera">bombed an Iraqi nuclear materials testing reactor at Osirak</a> and was widely criticised at the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> During the eight-year war with Iran, Saddam Hussein extensively used <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_chemical_weapons_program" title="Iraqi chemical weapons program">chemical weapons</a> against Iranians.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In the final stages of the Iran–Iraq War, the Ba'athist Iraqi regime led the <a href="/wiki/Al-Anfal_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Anfal Campaign">Al-Anfal Campaign</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Genocidal" class="mw-redirect" title="Genocidal">genocidal</a><sup id="cite_ref-hrw.org_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hrw.org-77">[77]</a></sup> campaign that targeted Iraqi Kurds,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and led to the killing of 50,000–100,000 civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Saddam_Hussein_and_Hassan_al-Bakr_1978.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Saddam_Hussein_and_Hassan_al-Bakr_1978.jpg/220px-Saddam_Hussein_and_Hassan_al-Bakr_1978.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Saddam_Hussein_and_Hassan_al-Bakr_1978.jpg/330px-Saddam_Hussein_and_Hassan_al-Bakr_1978.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/Saddam_Hussein_and_Hassan_al-Bakr_1978.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="290"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Saddam_Hussein_and_Hassan_al-Bakr_1978.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Ba'athist era presidents Hassan al-Bakr and Saddam Hussein in 1978.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In August 1990, <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Kuwait" title="Invasion of Kuwait">Iraq invaded and annexed Kuwait</a>. This subsequently led to <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">military intervention</a> by <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>-led forces in the First <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>. The coalition forces proceeded with a bombing campaign targeting military targets<sup id="cite_ref-Crusade_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crusade-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arbuthnot_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arbuthnot-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CSM2002_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSM2002-84">[84]</a></sup> and then launched a 100-hour-long ground assault against Iraqi forces in Southern Iraq and those occupying Kuwait. </p>
      <p>Iraq's armed forces were devastated during the war. Shortly after it ended in 1991, <a href="/wiki/Kurds_in_Iraq" title="Kurds in Iraq">Kurdish Iraqis</a> led <a href="/wiki/1991_uprisings_in_Iraq" title="1991 uprisings in Iraq">several uprisings</a> against Saddam Hussein's regime, but these were successfully repressed using the Iraqi security forces and chemical weapons. It is estimated that as many as 100,000 people, including many civilians were killed.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> During the uprisings the US, UK, France and Turkey, claiming authority under <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_688" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 688">UNSCR 688</a>, established the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_no-fly_zones" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi no-fly zones">Iraqi no-fly zones</a> to protect Kurdish population from attacks by the Saddam regime's fixed-wing aircraft (but not helicopters). </p>
      <p>Iraq was ordered to destroy its chemical and biological weapons and the UN attempted to compel Saddam's government to disarm and agree to a ceasefire by imposing additional sanctions on the country in addition to the initial sanctions imposed following Iraq's invasion of Kuwait. The Iraqi Government's failure to disarm and agree to a ceasefire resulted in <a href="/wiki/Iraq_sanctions" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraq sanctions">sanctions</a> which remained in place until 2003. The effects of the sanctions on the civilian population of Iraq have been disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-unicef99_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unicef99-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rubin_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin-87">[87]</a></sup> Whereas it was widely believed that the sanctions caused a major rise in child mortality, recent research has shown that commonly cited data were fabricated by the Iraqi government and that "there was no major rise in child mortality in Iraq after 1990 and during the period of the sanctions."<sup id="cite_ref-Spagat_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spagat-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Oil_for_food_program" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil for food program">oil for food program</a> was established in 1996 to ease the effects of sanctions. </p>
      <p>Following the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_W._Bush" title="Presidency of George W. Bush">George W. Bush administration</a> began planning the overthrow of Saddam's government and in October 2002, the US Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Resolution_to_Authorize_the_Use_of_United_States_Armed_Forces_Against_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Joint Resolution to Authorize the Use of United States Armed Forces Against Iraq">Joint Resolution to Authorize the Use of United States Armed Forces Against Iraq</a>. In November 2002, the UN Security Council passed <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1441" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1441">UNSCR 1441</a> and in March 2003 the US and its allies invaded Iraq. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span></h4> 
      <h5><span id="2003.E2.80.932007:_Invasion_and_occupation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2003–2007:_Invasion_and_occupation">2003–2007: Invasion and occupation</span></h5> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:SaddamStatue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/SaddamStatue.jpg/170px-SaddamStatue.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/SaddamStatue.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="252" data-file-height="303"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:SaddamStatue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The April 2003 toppling of <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a>'s statue by US Army troops in <a href="/wiki/Firdos_Square" title="Firdos Square">Firdos Square</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a> invasion.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iraq_(2003%E2%80%9311)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Iraq (2003–11)">History of Iraq (2003–11)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>
      </div> 
      <p>On 20 March 2003, a United States-organized coalition <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">invaded Iraq</a>, under the pretext that Iraq had failed to abandon its <a href="/wiki/Iraq_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Iraq and weapons of mass destruction">weapons of mass destruction program</a> in violation of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_687" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 687">UN Resolution 687</a>. This claim was based on documents provided by the <a href="/wiki/CIA" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA">CIA</a> and the British government that were later <a href="/wiki/Duelfer_Report" class="mw-redirect" title="Duelfer Report">found to be unreliable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Following the invasion, the United States established the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_Provisional_Authority" title="Coalition Provisional Authority">Coalition Provisional Authority</a> to govern Iraq. In May 2003 <a href="/wiki/L._Paul_Bremer" class="mw-redirect" title="L. Paul Bremer">L. Paul Bremer</a>, the chief executive of the CPA, issued orders to <a href="/wiki/De-Ba%27athification" title="De-Ba'athification">exclude Baath Party members</a> from the new Iraqi government (CPA Order 1) and to disband the Iraqi Army (<a href="/wiki/Coalition_Provisional_Authority_Order_2" title="Coalition Provisional Authority Order 2">CPA Order 2</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The decision dissolved the largely Sunni Iraqi Army and excluded many of the country's former government officials from participating in the country's governance,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> including 40,000 school teachers who had joined the Baath Party simply to keep their jobs,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> helping to bring about a chaotic post-invasion environment.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Iraqi_insurgency_(2003%E2%80%9306)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi insurgency (2003–06)">An insurgency</a> against the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">US-led coalition</a>-rule of Iraq began in summer 2003 within elements of the former Iraqi secret police and army, who formed guerrilla units. In fall 2003, self-entitled '<a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihadist</a>' groups began targeting coalition forces. Various Sunni militias were created in 2003, for example <a href="/wiki/Jama%27at_al-Tawhid_wal-Jihad" title="Jama'at al-Tawhid wal-Jihad">Jama'at al-Tawhid wal-Jihad</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Abu_Musab_al-Zarqawi" title="Abu Musab al-Zarqawi">Abu Musab al-Zarqawi</a>. The insurgency included intense inter-ethnic violence between Sunnis and Shias.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn.com_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn.com-98">[98]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Abu_Ghraib_torture_and_prisoner_abuse" title="Abu Ghraib torture and prisoner abuse">Abu Ghraib torture and prisoner abuse</a> scandal came to light, late 2003 in reports by <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> and <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_031016-N-3236B-043_A_marine_patrols_the_streets_of_Al_Faw,_Iraq.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/US_Navy_031016-N-3236B-043_A_marine_patrols_the_streets_of_Al_Faw%2C_Iraq.jpg/220px-US_Navy_031016-N-3236B-043_A_marine_patrols_the_streets_of_Al_Faw%2C_Iraq.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/US_Navy_031016-N-3236B-043_A_marine_patrols_the_streets_of_Al_Faw%2C_Iraq.jpg/330px-US_Navy_031016-N-3236B-043_A_marine_patrols_the_streets_of_Al_Faw%2C_Iraq.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/US_Navy_031016-N-3236B-043_A_marine_patrols_the_streets_of_Al_Faw%2C_Iraq.jpg/440px-US_Navy_031016-N-3236B-043_A_marine_patrols_the_streets_of_Al_Faw%2C_Iraq.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="1960"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_031016-N-3236B-043_A_marine_patrols_the_streets_of_Al_Faw,_Iraq.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">US Marines</a> patrol the streets of <a href="/wiki/Al_Faw" class="mw-redirect" title="Al Faw">Al Faw</a>, October 2003.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Mahdi_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahdi Army">Mahdi Army</a>—a Shia militia created in the summer of 2003 by <a href="/wiki/Muqtada_al-Sadr" title="Muqtada al-Sadr">Muqtada al-Sadr</a>—began to fight Coalition forces in April 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-mehdi_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mehdi-99">[99]</a></sup> 2004 saw Sunni and Shia militants fighting against each other and against the new <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Interim_Government" title="Iraqi Interim Government">Iraqi Interim Government</a> installed in June 2004, and against Coalition forces, as well as the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Fallujah" title="First Battle of Fallujah">First Battle of Fallujah</a> in April and <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Fallujah" title="Second Battle of Fallujah">Second Battle of Fallujah</a> in November. The Madhi army would kidnap Sunni civilians as part of a genocide that occurred against them.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In January 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_parliamentary_election,_January_2005" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi parliamentary election, January 2005">first elections</a> since the invasion took place and in October a <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Iraq" title="Constitution of Iraq">new Constitution</a> was approved,<sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-1">[1]</a></sup> which was followed by <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_parliamentary_election,_December_2005" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi parliamentary election, December 2005">parliamentary elections</a> in December. However, insurgent attacks were common and increased to 34,131 in 2005 from 26,496 in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During 2006, fighting continued and reached its highest levels of violence, more <a href="/wiki/Haditha_killings" class="mw-redirect" title="Haditha killings">war crimes scandals</a> were made public, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Musab_al-Zarqawi" title="Abu Musab al-Zarqawi">Abu Musab al-Zarqawi</a> the leader of <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda_in_Iraq" title="Al-Qaeda in Iraq">Al-Qaeda in Iraq</a> was killed by US forces and Iraq's former dictator <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> was sentenced to death for <a href="/wiki/Crimes_against_humanity" title="Crimes against humanity">crimes against humanity</a> and hanged.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> In late 2006, the US government's <a href="/wiki/Iraq_Study_Group" title="Iraq Study Group">Iraq Study Group</a> recommended that the US begin focusing on training Iraqi military personnel and in January 2007 US President George W. Bush announced a <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War_troop_surge_of_2007" title="Iraq War troop surge of 2007">"Surge"</a> in the number of US troops deployed to the country.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In May 2007, Iraq's Parliament called on the United States to set a timetable for withdrawal and US coalition partners such as the UK and Denmark began withdrawing their forces from the country.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The war in Iraq has resulted in <a href="/wiki/Casualties_of_the_Iraq_War" title="Casualties of the Iraq War">between 151,000 and 1.2 million Iraqis being killed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h5><span id="2008.E2.80.932018:_Instability_and_ISIS"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2008–2018:_Instability_and_ISIS">2008–2018: Instability and ISIS</span></h5> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main page: <a href="/wiki/Category:2010s_in_Iraq" title="Category:2010s in Iraq">2010s in Iraq</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Civil_War_(2014%E2%80%932017)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Civil War (2014–2017)">Iraqi Civil War (2014–2017)</a>, <a href="/wiki/American-led_intervention_in_Iraq_(2014%E2%80%93present)" title="American-led intervention in Iraq (2014–present)">American-led intervention in Iraq (2014–present)</a>, <a href="/wiki/2017_Iraqi%E2%80%93Kurdish_conflict" title="2017 Iraqi–Kurdish conflict">2017 Iraqi–Kurdish conflict</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_insurgency_(2017%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi insurgency (2017–present)">Iraqi insurgency (2017–present)</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Iraq_spring_fighting_of_2008" title="Iraq spring fighting of 2008">fighting continued</a> and Iraq's newly trained armed forces launched attacks against militants. The Iraqi government signed the <a href="/wiki/US%E2%80%93Iraq_Status_of_Forces_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="US–Iraq Status of Forces Agreement">US–Iraq Status of Forces Agreement</a>, which required US forces to withdraw from Iraqi cities by 30 June 2009 and to withdraw completely from Iraq by 31 December 2011. </p>
      <p>US troops handed over security duties to Iraqi forces in June 2009, though they continued to work with Iraqi forces after the pullout.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> On the morning of 18 December 2011, the final contingent of US troops to be withdrawn ceremonially exited over the border to <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn-dec11_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-dec11-14">[14]</a></sup> Crime and violence initially spiked in the months following <a href="/wiki/U.S.%E2%80%93Iraq_Status_of_Forces_Agreement" title="U.S.–Iraq Status of Forces Agreement">the US withdrawal from cities in mid-2009</a><sup id="cite_ref-The_Associated_Press_and_Yahoo.com_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Associated_Press_and_Yahoo.com-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> but despite the initial increase in violence, in November 2009, Iraqi <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Interior_(Iraq)" title="Ministry of Interior (Iraq)">Interior Ministry</a> officials reported that the civilian death toll in Iraq fell to its lowest level since the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Islamic_State_Militant_Activity_In_Iraq_Syria_and_Lebanon_VOA.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Islamic_State_Militant_Activity_In_Iraq_Syria_and_Lebanon_VOA.png/220px-Islamic_State_Militant_Activity_In_Iraq_Syria_and_Lebanon_VOA.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Islamic_State_Militant_Activity_In_Iraq_Syria_and_Lebanon_VOA.png/330px-Islamic_State_Militant_Activity_In_Iraq_Syria_and_Lebanon_VOA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Islamic_State_Militant_Activity_In_Iraq_Syria_and_Lebanon_VOA.png/440px-Islamic_State_Militant_Activity_In_Iraq_Syria_and_Lebanon_VOA.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="640"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Islamic_State_Militant_Activity_In_Iraq_Syria_and_Lebanon_VOA.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Military situation in 2015
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_of_U.S._troops_from_Iraq_(2007%E2%80%932011)" title="Withdrawal of U.S. troops from Iraq (2007–2011)">withdrawal of US troops</a> in 2011, the insurgency continued and Iraq suffered from political instability. In February 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> protests <a href="/wiki/2011_Iraqi_protests" title="2011 Iraqi protests">spread to Iraq</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> but the initial protests did not topple the government. The <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_National_Movement" title="Iraqi National Movement">Iraqi National Movement</a>, reportedly representing the majority of Iraqi Sunnis, boycotted Parliament for several weeks in late 2011 and early 2012, claiming that the Shiite-dominated government was striving to sideline Sunnis. </p>
      <p>In 2012 and 2013, levels of violence increased and armed groups inside Iraq were increasingly galvanised by the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>. Both Sunnis and Shias crossed the border to fight in Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurd-Shiite-Sunni-Split_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurd-Shiite-Sunni-Split-116">[116]</a></sup> In December 2012, Sunni Arabs <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%932013_Iraqi_protests" title="2012–2013 Iraqi protests">protested</a> against the government, whom they claimed marginalised them.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During 2013, Sunni militant groups stepped up attacks targeting the Iraq's population in an attempt to undermine confidence in the <a href="/wiki/Nouri_al-Maliki" title="Nouri al-Maliki">Nouri al-Maliki</a>-led government.<sup id="cite_ref-latimes2701_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latimes2701-119">[119]</a></sup> In 2014, Sunni insurgents belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> (ISIL) terrorist group seized control of large swathes of land including several major Iraqi cities, like <a href="/wiki/Tikrit" title="Tikrit">Tikrit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fallujah" title="Fallujah">Fallujah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a> creating hundreds of thousands of <a href="/wiki/Internally_displaced_persons" class="mw-redirect" title="Internally displaced persons">internally displaced persons</a> amid reports of atrocities by ISIL fighters.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After an inconclusive election in April 2014, Nouri al-Maliki served as caretaker-Prime-Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-guar11-8-14_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guar11-8-14-121">[121]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 11 August, Iraq's highest court ruled that PM Maliki's bloc was the largest in parliament, meaning Maliki could stay Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-guar11-8-14_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guar11-8-14-121">[121]</a></sup> By 13 August, however, the Iraqi president had tasked <a href="/wiki/Haider_al-Abadi" title="Haider al-Abadi">Haider al-Abadi</a> with forming a new government, and the United Nations, the United States, the European Union, Saudi Arabia, Iran, and some Iraqi politicians expressed their wish for a new leadership in Iraq, for example from <a href="/wiki/Haider_al-Abadi" title="Haider al-Abadi">Haider al-Abadi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> On 14 August, Maliki stepped down as PM to support Mr al-Abadi and to "safeguard the high interests of the country". The US government welcomed this as "another major step forward" in uniting Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> On 9 September 2014, <a href="/wiki/Haider_al-Abadi" title="Haider al-Abadi">Haider al-Abadi</a> had formed a new government and became the new prime minister.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Intermittent conflict between Sunni, <a href="/wiki/Shiite" class="mw-redirect" title="Shiite">Shiite</a> and Kurdish factions has led to increasing debate about the splitting of Iraq into three autonomous regions, including Sunni Kurdistan in the northeast, a <a href="/wiki/Sunnistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunnistan">Sunnistan</a> in the west and a Shiastan in the southeast.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In response to rapid territorial gains made by the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> (ISIL) during the first half of 2014, and its universally-condemned <a href="/wiki/Killing_of_captives_by_ISIL" title="Killing of captives by ISIL">executions</a> and reported <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant#Human_rights_abuses" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">human rights abuses</a>, many states <a href="/wiki/International_military_intervention_against_ISIL" title="International military intervention against ISIL">began to intervene</a> against it in the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Civil_War_(2014%E2%80%932017)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Civil War (2014–2017)">Iraqi Civil War (2014–2017)</a>. Since the airstrikes started, ISIL has been losing ground in both Iraq and Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Tens of thousands of civilians have been killed in Iraq in ISIL-linked violence.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Genocide_of_Yazidis_by_ISIL" title="Genocide of Yazidis by ISIL">genocide of Yazidis by ISIL</a> has led to the expulsion, flight and effective exile of the <a href="/wiki/Yazidis" title="Yazidis">Yazidis</a> from their ancestral lands in northern Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2016_Karrada_bombing" title="2016 Karrada bombing">2016 Karrada bombing</a> killed nearly 400 civilians and injured hundreds more.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> On 17 March 2017, a US-led coalition <a href="/wiki/2017_Mosul_airstrike" class="mw-redirect" title="2017 Mosul airstrike">airstrike in Mosul</a> killed more than 200 civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since 2015, ISIL lost territory in Iraq, including <a href="/wiki/Tikrit" title="Tikrit">Tikrit</a> in March and April 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baiji,_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Baiji, Iraq">Baiji</a> in October 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-US_praise_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US_praise-133">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sinjar" title="Sinjar">Sinjar</a> in November 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ramadi" title="Ramadi">Ramadi</a> in December 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fallujah" title="Fallujah">Fallujah</a> in June 2016<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a> in July 2017. By December 2017, ISIL had no remaining territory in Iraq, following the <a href="/wiki/2017_Western_Iraq_campaign" title="2017 Western Iraq campaign">2017 Western Iraq campaign</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-victory_parade_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-victory_parade-137">[137]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In September 2017, a <a href="/wiki/2017_Kurdistan_Region_independence_referendum" title="2017 Kurdistan Region independence referendum">referendum</a> was held regarding <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_nationalism" title="Kurdish nationalism">Kurdish independence</a> in Iraq. 92% of Iraqi Kurds voted in favor of independence.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The referendum was regarded as illegal by the federal government in Baghdad.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> In March 2018, Turkey launched <a href="/wiki/Operation_Tigris_Shield" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Tigris Shield">military operations</a> to eliminate the Kurdish separatist fighters in northern Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Anti-American cleric <a href="/wiki/Muqtada_al-Sadr" title="Muqtada al-Sadr">Muqtada al-Sadr</a>'s political coalition won <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_parliamentary_election,_2018" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi parliamentary election, 2018">Iraq's parliamentary election</a> in May 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h5><span id="2019.E2.80.93present:_Civil_unrest_and_proxy_war"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2019–present:_Civil_unrest_and_proxy_war">2019–present: Civil unrest and proxy war</span></h5> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main page: <a href="/wiki/Category:2020s_in_Iraq" title="Category:2020s in Iraq">2020s in Iraq</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Serious civil unrest rocked the country beginning in Baghdad and Najaf in July 2018 and spreading to other provinces in <a href="/wiki/2018%E2%80%9319_Iraqi_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2018–19 Iraqi protests">late September 2019</a> as rallies to protest corruption, unemployment, and public service failures turned violent.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_protests_(2019%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi protests (2019–present)">Protests and demonstrations started again on 1 October 2019</a>, against 16 years of corruption, unemployment and inefficient public services, before they escalated into calls to overthrow the administration and to stop <a href="/wiki/Iranian_intervention_in_Iraq_(2014%E2%80%93present)" title="Iranian intervention in Iraq (2014–present)">Iranian intervention in Iraq</a>. The Iraqi government at times reacted harshly, resulting in over 500 deaths by 12 December 2019. </p>
      <p>On 27 December 2019, the <a href="/wiki/K-1_Air_Base_attack" class="mw-redirect" title="K-1 Air Base attack">K-1 Air Base in Iraq was attacked</a> by more than 30 rockets, killing a U.S. civilian contractor and injuring others. The U.S. blamed the Iranian-backed <a href="/wiki/Kata%27ib_Hezbollah" title="Kata'ib Hezbollah">Kata'ib Hezbollah</a> militia. Later that month, the <a href="/wiki/December_2019_United_States_airstrikes_in_Iraq_and_Syria" title="December 2019 United States airstrikes in Iraq and Syria">United States bombed five Kata'ib Hezbollah militia's positions in Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, in retaliation for the presumed Kata'ib attack of 27 December. According to Iraqi sources, at least 25 militia fighters were killed. On 31 December 2019, after a funeral for <a href="/wiki/Kata%27ib_Hezbollah" title="Kata'ib Hezbollah">Kata'ib Hezbollah</a> militiamen killed by U.S. airstrikes, dozens of Iraqi Shia militiamen and their supporters marched into the <a href="/wiki/Green_Zone" title="Green Zone">Green Zone</a> of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> and surrounded the U.S. embassy compound (see article: <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_the_United_States_embassy_in_Baghdad" title="Attack on the United States embassy in Baghdad">Attack on the United States embassy in Baghdad</a>). Demonstrators smashed a door of the checkpoint, set fire to the reception area, left anti-American posters and sprayed anti-American graffiti. U.S. president Trump accused Iran of orchestrating the attack. </p>
      <p>On 3 January 2020, amid <a href="/wiki/2019%E2%80%9320_Persian_Gulf_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2019–20 Persian Gulf crisis">rising tensions between the United States and Iran</a>, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/2020_Baghdad_International_Airport_airstrike" class="mw-redirect" title="2020 Baghdad International Airport airstrike">launched a drone strike on a convoy traveling near Baghdad International Airport</a>, killing <a href="/wiki/Qasem_Soleimani" title="Qasem Soleimani">Qasem Soleimani</a>, Iranian major general and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Revolutionary_Guard_Corps" title="Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps">Islamic Revolutionary Guard Corps</a> (IRGC) and <a href="/wiki/Quds_Force" title="Quds Force">Quds Force</a> commander, the second most powerful person of Iran;<sup id="cite_ref-nrc3Jan_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrc3Jan-143">[143]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mahdi_al-Muhandis" title="Abu Mahdi al-Muhandis">Abu Mahdi al-Muhandis</a>, deputy commander of Iraq's <a href="/wiki/Popular_Mobilization_Forces" title="Popular Mobilization Forces">Popular Mobilization Forces</a> (PMF or PMU), four senior Iranian officers; and four Iraqi officers. </p>
      <p>Following months of protests that broke out across Iraq in October 2019 and the resignation of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Adil_Abdul-Mahdi" title="Adil Abdul-Mahdi">Adel Abdul Mahdi</a> and his cabinet, Mustafa Al Kadhimi became a leading contender for the Premiership.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> On 9 April 2020, he was named by <a href="/wiki/President_of_Iraq" title="President of Iraq">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Barham_Salih" title="Barham Salih">Barham Salih</a> as <a href="/wiki/Prime_minister-designate" title="Prime minister-designate">prime minister-designate</a>, the third person tapped to lead the country in just 10 weeks as it struggled to replace a government that fell last year after months of <a href="/wiki/2019%E2%80%9320_Iraqi_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2019–20 Iraqi protests">protests</a>. Kadhimi was nominated by President Barham Salih, state television reported, shortly after the previous designated <a href="/wiki/Prime_minister_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime minister of Iraq">prime minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adnan_al-Zurfi" title="Adnan al-Zurfi">Adnan al-Zurfi</a>, announced he was withdrawing having failed to secure enough support to pass a <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Government of Iraq">government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">

      <p>Iraq lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/29th_parallel_north" title="29th parallel north">29°</a> and <a href="/wiki/38th_parallel_north" title="38th parallel north">38° N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/39th_meridian_east" title="39th meridian east">39°</a> and <a href="/wiki/49th_meridian_east" title="49th meridian east">49° E</a> (a small area lies west of 39°). Spanning 437,072&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (168,754&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi), it is the 58th-largest country in the world. It is comparable in size to the US state of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, and somewhat larger than <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>. </p>
      <p>Iraq mainly consists of <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a>, but near the two major rivers (<a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>) are fertile <a href="/wiki/Alluvial_plains" class="mw-redirect" title="Alluvial plains">alluvial plains</a>, as the rivers carry about 60,000,000&nbsp;m<sup>3</sup> (78,477,037&nbsp;cu&nbsp;yd) of <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a> annually to the <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">delta</a>. The north of the country is mostly composed of mountains; the highest point being at 3,611&nbsp;m (11,847&nbsp;ft) point, unnamed on the map opposite, but known locally as <a href="/wiki/Cheekah_Dar" class="mw-redirect" title="Cheekah Dar">Cheekah Dar</a> (black tent). Iraq has a small coastline measuring 58&nbsp;km (36&nbsp;mi) along the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>. Close to the coast and along the <a href="/wiki/Shatt_al-Arab" title="Shatt al-Arab">Shatt al-Arab</a> (known as <i>arvandrūd</i>: اروندرود among Iranians) there used to be marshlands, but many were drained in the 1990s. </p>
      <p>Iraq is home to seven terrestrial ecoregions: <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains_forest_steppe" title="Zagros Mountains forest steppe">Zagros Mountains forest steppe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syrian_xeric_grasslands_and_shrublands" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian xeric grasslands and shrublands">Syrian xeric grasslands and shrublands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tigris-Euphrates_alluvial_salt_marsh" class="mw-redirect" title="Tigris-Euphrates alluvial salt marsh">Tigris-Euphrates alluvial salt marsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean_conifer-sclerophyllous-broadleaf_forests" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Mediterranean conifer-sclerophyllous-broadleaf forests">Eastern Mediterranean conifer-sclerophyllous-broadleaf forests</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Desert" title="Arabian Desert">Arabian Desert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_shrub_desert" title="Mesopotamian shrub desert">Mesopotamian shrub desert</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_Iran_Nubo-Sindian_desert_and_semi-desert" class="mw-redirect" title="South Iran Nubo-Sindian desert and semi-desert">South Iran Nubo-Sindian desert and semi-desert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DinersteinOlson2017_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DinersteinOlson2017-146">[146]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Iraq#Climate" title="Geography of Iraq">Geography of Iraq §&nbsp;Climate</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Most of Iraq has a hot <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> climate with <a href="/wiki/Subtropical" class="mw-redirect" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> influence. Summer temperatures average above 40&nbsp;°C (104&nbsp;°F) for most of the country and frequently exceed 48&nbsp;°C (118.4&nbsp;°F). Winter temperatures infrequently exceed 21&nbsp;°C (69.8&nbsp;°F) with maxima roughly 15 to 19&nbsp;°C (59.0 to 66.2&nbsp;°F) and night-time lows 2 to 5&nbsp;°C (35.6 to 41.0&nbsp;°F). Typically, precipitation is low; most places receive less than 250&nbsp;mm (9.8&nbsp;in) annually, with maximum rainfall occurring during the winter months. Rainfall during the summer is extremely rare, except in the far north of the country. The northern mountainous regions have cold winters with occasional heavy snows, sometimes causing extensive flooding. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_Iraq" title="Climate change in Iraq">Climate change in Iraq</a> is leading to increasing temperatures, reduced precipitation, and increasing <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity" title="Water scarcity">water scarcity</a> which will likely have serious implications for the country for years to come.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Iraq" title="Politics of Iraq">Politics of Iraq</a>
      </div>

       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

         </div>Baghdad Convention Center, the current meeting place of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Representatives_of_Iraq" title="Council of Representatives of Iraq">Council of Representatives of Iraq</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_Iraq" title="Federal government of Iraq">federal government of Iraq</a> is defined under the current <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Iraq" title="Constitution of Iraq">Constitution</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">democratic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federal</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>. The federal government is composed of the <a href="/wiki/Executive_branch" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive branch">executive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legislative_branch" class="mw-redirect" title="Legislative branch">legislative</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Judicial_branch" class="mw-redirect" title="Judicial branch">judicial</a> branches, as well as numerous independent commissions. Aside from the federal government, there are regions (made of one or more governorates), governorates, and districts within Iraq with jurisdiction over various matters as defined by law.<sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-1">[1]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Alliance_(Iraq)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Alliance (Iraq)">National Alliance</a> is the main Shia parliamentary bloc, and was established as a result of a merger of Prime Minister Nouri Maliki's <a href="/wiki/State_of_Law_Coalition" title="State of Law Coalition">State of Law Coalition</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_National_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi National Alliance">Iraqi National Alliance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-gpi_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-gpi-148">[148]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_National_Movement" title="Iraqi National Movement">Iraqi National Movement</a> is led by <a href="/wiki/Iyad_Allawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Iyad Allawi">Iyad Allawi</a>, a secular Shia widely supported by Sunnis. The party has a more consistent anti-sectarian perspective than most of its rivals.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-gpi_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-gpi-148">[148]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_List" title="Kurdistan List">Kurdistan List</a> is dominated by two parties, the <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Democratic_Party_(Iraq)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdistan Democratic Party (Iraq)">Kurdistan Democratic Party</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Masood_Barzani" class="mw-redirect" title="Masood Barzani">Masood Barzani</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Patriotic_Union_of_Kurdistan" title="Patriotic Union of Kurdistan">Patriotic Union of Kurdistan</a> headed by <a href="/wiki/Jalal_Talabani" title="Jalal Talabani">Jalal Talabani</a>. Both parties are secular and enjoy close ties with the West.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-gpi_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-gpi-148">[148]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2008, according to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Failed_States_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by Failed States Index">Failed States Index</a>, Iraq was the world's eleventh most politically unstable country.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The concentration of power in the hands of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Nouri_al-Maliki" title="Nouri al-Maliki">Nouri al-Maliki</a> and growing pressure on the opposition led to growing concern about the future of political rights in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-fh13_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fh13-151">[151]</a></sup> Nevertheless, progress was made and the country had risen to 11th place by 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> In August 2014, al-Maliki's reign came to an end. He announced on 14 August 2014 that he would stand aside so that <a href="/wiki/Haider_Al-Abadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Haider Al-Abadi">Haider Al-Abadi</a>, who had been nominated just days earlier by newly installed President <a href="/wiki/Fuad_Masum" title="Fuad Masum">Fuad Masum</a>, could take over. Until that point, al-Maliki had clung to power even asking the federal court to veto the president's nomination describing it as a violation of the constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-NewPM_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewPM-153">[153]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Transparency International ranks Iraq's government as the eighth-most-corrupt government in the world. Government payroll have increased from 1 million employees under <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> to around 7 million employees in 2016. In combination with decreased oil prices, the government budget deficit is near 25% of GDP as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Iraq&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Pre-referendum,_pro-Kurdistan,_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil,_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg/220px-Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg/330px-Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg/440px-Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Pre-referendum,_pro-Kurdistan,_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil,_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Pro-independence rally in <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Region" title="Kurdistan Region">Kurdistan Region</a> in September 2017
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Since the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_no-fly_zones" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi no-fly zones">no–fly zones</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a> of 1990–1991, the Kurds established their own <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous area">autonomous region</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Iraq" title="Law of Iraq">Law of Iraq</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In October 2005, the new <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Iraq" title="Constitution of Iraq">Constitution of Iraq</a> was approved in a referendum with a 78% overall majority, although the percentage of support varying widely between the country's territories.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The new constitution was backed by the Shia and Kurdish communities, but was rejected by Arab Sunnis. Under the terms of the constitution, the country conducted <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_parliamentary_election,_December_2005" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi parliamentary election, December 2005">fresh nationwide parliamentary elections</a> on 15 December 2005. All three major <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups in Iraq">ethnic groups in Iraq</a> voted along ethnic lines, as did Assyrian and Turcoman minorities. </p>
      <p>Law no. 188 of the year 1959 (Personal Status Law)<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> made polygamy extremely difficult, granted child custody to the mother in case of divorce, prohibited repudiation and marriage under the age of 16.<sup id="cite_ref-unesco1_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unesco1-157">[157]</a></sup> Article 1 of Civil Code also identifies Islamic law as a formal source of law.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Iraq had no Sharia courts but civil courts used Sharia for issues of personal status including marriage and divorce. In 1995 Iraq introduced Sharia punishment for certain types of criminal offences.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> The code is based on French civil law as well as Sunni and Jafari (<a href="/wiki/Shi%27ite" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi'ite">Shi'ite</a>) interpretations of Sharia.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_Provisional_Authority" title="Coalition Provisional Authority">CPA</a> chief executive L. Paul Bremer said he would veto any constitutional draft stating that sharia is the principal basis of law.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> The declaration enraged many local Shia clerics,<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> and by 2005 the United States had relented, allowing a role for sharia in the constitution to help end a stalemate on the draft constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Penal_Code" title="Iraqi Penal Code">Iraqi Penal Code</a> is the statutory law of Iraq. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Armed_Forces" title="Iraqi Armed Forces">Iraqi Armed Forces</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Iraqi_Civil_War_map_(2014%E2%80%93present).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Iraqi_Civil_War_map_%282014%E2%80%93present%29.png/170px-Iraqi_Civil_War_map_%282014%E2%80%93present%29.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Iraqi_Civil_War_map_%282014%E2%80%93present%29.png/255px-Iraqi_Civil_War_map_%282014%E2%80%93present%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Iraqi_Civil_War_map_%282014%E2%80%93present%29.png/340px-Iraqi_Civil_War_map_%282014%E2%80%93present%29.png 2x" data-file-width="3360" data-file-height="3436"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Iraq_war_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The current military control in Iraq as of 3 May 2018:
         <br>
         <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style>
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#db8ca6; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Controlled by <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_Iraq" title="Federal government of Iraq">Iraqi government</a>
         </div>
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959">
         <div class="legend">
          <span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#d7e074; color:black;">&nbsp;</span>&nbsp;Controlled by <a href="/wiki/Peshmerga" title="Peshmerga">Iraqi Kurds</a>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Iraqi security forces are composed of forces serving under the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Interior" class="mw-redirect" title="Ministry of Interior">Ministry of Interior</a> (which controls the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Police" title="Iraqi Police">Police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Popular_Mobilization_Forces" title="Popular Mobilization Forces">Popular Mobilization Forces</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(Iraq)" title="Ministry of Defence (Iraq)">Ministry of Defense</a>, as well as the Iraqi <a href="/wiki/Counter_Terrorism_Bureau" class="mw-redirect" title="Counter Terrorism Bureau">Counter Terrorism Bureau</a>, reporting directly to the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Iraq" title="Prime Minister of Iraq">Prime Minister of Iraq</a>, which oversees the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Special_Operations_Forces" title="Iraqi Special Operations Forces">Iraqi Special Operations Forces</a>. Ministry of Defense forces include the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">Iraqi Army</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Air_Force" title="Iraqi Air Force">Iraqi Air Force</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Navy" title="Iraqi Navy">Iraqi Navy</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Peshmerga" title="Peshmerga">Peshmerga</a> are a separate armed force loyal to the <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Regional_Government" title="Kurdistan Regional Government">Kurdistan Regional Government</a>. The regional government and the central government disagree as to whether they are under Baghdad's authority and to what extent.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">Iraqi Army</a> is an objective counter-insurgency force that as of November 2009 includes 14 divisions, each division consisting of 4 brigades.<sup id="cite_ref-ltcolswanson-040308_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ltcolswanson-040308-165">[165]</a></sup> It is described as the most important element of the counter-insurgency fight.<sup id="cite_ref-The_New_Iraqi_Security_Forces_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_Iraqi_Security_Forces-166">[166]</a></sup> Light infantry brigades are equipped with small arms, machine guns, RPGs, body armour and light armoured vehicles. Mechanized infantry brigades are equipped with <a href="/wiki/T-54/55" class="mw-redirect" title="T-54/55">T-54/55</a> main battle tanks and <a href="/wiki/BMP-1" title="BMP-1">BMP-1</a> infantry fighting vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-The_New_Iraqi_Security_Forces_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_Iraqi_Security_Forces-166">[166]</a></sup> As of mid-2008, logistical problems included a maintenance crisis and ongoing supply problems.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Iraqi_T-72_tank_fires.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Iraqi_T-72_tank_fires.JPG/220px-Iraqi_T-72_tank_fires.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Iraqi_T-72_tank_fires.JPG/330px-Iraqi_T-72_tank_fires.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Iraqi_T-72_tank_fires.JPG/440px-Iraqi_T-72_tank_fires.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4173" data-file-height="2463"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Iraqi_T-72_tank_fires.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Iraqi T-72 tank fires.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Air_Force" title="Iraqi Air Force">Iraqi Air Force</a> is designed to support ground forces with surveillance, reconnaissance and troop lift. Two reconnaissance squadrons use light aircraft, three helicopter squadrons are used to move troops and one air transportation squadron uses C-130 transport aircraft to move troops, equipment, and supplies. It currently has 3,000 personnel. It is planned to increase to 18,000 personnel, with 550 aircraft by 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-The_New_Iraqi_Security_Forces_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_Iraqi_Security_Forces-166">[166]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Navy" title="Iraqi Navy">Iraqi Navy</a> is a small force with 1,500 sailors and officers, including 800 <a href="/wiki/Marines_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marines (military)">Marines</a>, designed to protect shoreline and inland waterways from insurgent infiltration. The navy is also responsible for the security of offshore oil platforms. The navy will have coastal patrol squadrons, assault boat squadrons and a <a href="/wiki/Marines_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marines (military)">marine</a> battalion.<sup id="cite_ref-The_New_Iraqi_Security_Forces_166-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_Iraqi_Security_Forces-166">[166]</a></sup> The force will consist of 2,000 to 2,500 sailors by year 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-WSR070321_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSR070321-168">[168]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 4 November 2019, more than 100 <a href="/wiki/Australian_Defence_Force" title="Australian Defence Force">Australian Defence Force</a> personnel left <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">Darwin</a> for the 10th rotation of Task Group <a href="/wiki/Taji" class="mw-redirect" title="Taji">Taji</a> base in north of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>. The Australian contingent mentors the Iraqi School of Infantry, where the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Security_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Security Forces">Iraqi Security Forces</a> are trained. However, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia's</a> contribution was reduced from 250 to 120 ADF personnel, which along with <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> had trained over 45,000 ISF members before that.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Iraq" title="Foreign relations of Iraq">Foreign relations of Iraq</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Haider_al-Abadi_and_Donald_Trump_in_the_Oval_Office,_March_2017.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Haider_al-Abadi_and_Donald_Trump_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_March_2017.jpg/220px-Haider_al-Abadi_and_Donald_Trump_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_March_2017.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Haider_al-Abadi_and_Donald_Trump_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_March_2017.jpg/330px-Haider_al-Abadi_and_Donald_Trump_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_March_2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Haider_al-Abadi_and_Donald_Trump_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_March_2017.jpg/440px-Haider_al-Abadi_and_Donald_Trump_in_the_Oval_Office%2C_March_2017.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1365"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Haider_al-Abadi_and_Donald_Trump_in_the_Oval_Office,_March_2017.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>US President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> with Iraqi Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Haider_al-Abadi" title="Haider al-Abadi">Haider al-Abadi</a> in 2017.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
       <tbody>

         <td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31"></a></td>
